           <span style="font-size: 120%;"><sup id="cite_ref-cia_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-11">[11]</a></sup></span> According to the CIA, estimates for this country explicitly take into account the effects of mortality because of AIDS; this can result in lower life expectancy, higher infant mortality and death rates, lower population and growth rates, and changes in the distribution of population by age and sex, than would otherwise be expected.

      <p><b>Kenya</b>, officially the <b>Republic of Kenya</b> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>: <i lang="sw">Jamhuri ya Kenya</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Africa">Eastern Africa</a>. At 580,367 square kilometres (224,081&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), Kenya is the world's 48th largest country by total area. With a population of more than 47.6 million people in the 2019 census,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Kenya is the 29th most populous country.<sup id="cite_ref-KNBS2019_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KNBS2019-6">[6]</a></sup> Kenya's capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, while its oldest city and first capital is the coastal city of <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kisumu" title="Kisumu">Kisumu City</a> is the third-largest city and also an inland port on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>. Other important urban centres include <a href="/wiki/Nakuru" title="Nakuru">Nakuru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eldoret" title="Eldoret">Eldoret</a>. As of 2020, Kenya is the third-largest economy in sub-Saharan Africa after Nigeria and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Kenya is bordered by <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the south, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to the southeast. </p>
      <p>According to archaeological dating of associated artifacts and skeletal material, the Cushites first settled in the lowlands of Kenya between 3,200 and 1,300 BC, a phase referred to as the Lowland <a href="/wiki/Savanna_Pastoral_Neolithic" title="Savanna Pastoral Neolithic">Savanna Pastoral Neolithic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Nilotic speakers) started migrating from present-day South Sudan into Kenya around 500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-14">[14]</a></sup> European contact began in 1500 with the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>, though effective <a href="/wiki/European_colonization" class="mw-redirect" title="European colonization">colonisation</a> of Kenya began in the 19th century during the <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of the interior</a>. Modern-day Kenya emerged from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">a protectorate</a> established by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in 1895 and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a>, which began in 1920. Numerous disputes between the UK and the colony led to the <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="Mau Mau uprising">Mau Mau revolution</a>, which began in 1952, and the subsequent declaration of independence in 1963. After independence, Kenya remained a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya" title="Constitution of Kenya">current constitution</a> was adopted in 2010 to replace the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kenya_(1963)" title="Constitution of Kenya (1963)">1963 independence constitution</a>. </p>
      <p>Kenya is a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic, in which elected officials represent the people and the president is the head of state and government.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Kenya is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">COMESA</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>, and other international organisations. With a <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Income" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross National Income">GNI</a> of 1,460,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Kenya is a lower-middle-income economy. <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Kenya" title="Economy of Kenya">Kenya's economy</a> is the largest in eastern and central Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-auto_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-18">[18]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a> serving as a major regional commercial hub.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-18">[18]</a></sup> Agriculture is the largest sector: tea and coffee are traditional cash crops, while fresh flowers are a fast-growing export. The <a href="/wiki/Service_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Service industry">service industry</a> is also a major economic driver, particularly tourism. Kenya is a member of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> trade bloc, though some international trade organisations categorise it as part of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Horn_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Horn of Africa">Greater Horn of Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Africa is Kenya's largest export market, followed by the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <p>The Republic of Kenya is named after <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenya" title="Mount Kenya">Mount Kenya</a>. The earliest recorded version of the modern name was written by German explorer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Johann Ludwig Krapf</a> in the 19th century. While travelling with a Kamba caravan led by the legendary long-distance trader Chief Kivoi, Krapf spotted the mountain peak and asked what it was called. Kivoi told him "<i>Kĩ-Nyaa</i>" or "<i>Kĩĩma- Kĩĩnyaa</i>", probably because the pattern of black rock and white snow on its peaks reminded him of the feathers of the male ostrich.<sup id="cite_ref-sullivan_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-21">[21]</a></sup> The Agikuyu, who inhabit the slopes of Mt. Kenya, call it Kĩrĩma Kĩrĩnyaga in <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_language" title="Kikuyu language">Kikuyu</a>, while the Embu call it "Kirenyaa". All three names have the same meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ludwig Krapf recorded the name as both <i>Kenia</i> and <i>Kegnia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Krapf_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krapf-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-krapf_452_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krapf_452-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-foottit_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foottit-25">[25]</a></sup> Some have said that this was a precise notation of the African pronunciation <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> An 1882 map drawn by Joseph Thompsons, a Scottish geologist and naturalist, indicated Mt. Kenya as Mt. Kenia.<sup id="cite_ref-sullivan_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sullivan-21">[21]</a></sup> The mountain's name was accepted, <a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto" title="Pars pro toto">pars pro toto</a>, as the name of the country. It did not come into widespread official use during the early colonial period, when the country was referred to as the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East African Protectorate</a>. The official name was changed to the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Colony of Kenya</a> in 1920. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kenya" title="History of Kenya">History of Kenya</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Kenya" title="Timeline of Kenya">Timeline of Kenya</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_Prehistory">Human Prehistory</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/140px-Turkana_Boy.jpg" decoding="async" width="140" height="399" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/210px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Turkana_Boy.jpg/280px-Turkana_Boy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="1437"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Turkana_Boy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Turkana_boy" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkana boy">Turkana boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old <a href="/wiki/Hominid" class="mw-redirect" title="Hominid">hominid</a> fossil belonging to <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Fossils found in Kenya have shown that primates inhabited the area for more than 20 million years. Recent findings near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> indicate that <a href="/wiki/Hominid" class="mw-redirect" title="Hominid">hominids</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i> (1.8 to 2.5 million years ago) and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> (1.9 million to 350,000 years ago) are possible direct ancestors of modern <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>, and lived in Kenya in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch.<sup id="cite_ref-Isaac_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During excavations at Lake Turkana in 1984, paleoanthropologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a>, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Kamoya_Kimeu" title="Kamoya Kimeu">Kamoya Kimeu</a>, discovered the <a href="/wiki/Turkana_Boy" title="Turkana Boy">Turkana Boy</a>, a 1.6-million-year-old <i>Homo erectus</i> fossil. Previous research on early hominids is particularly identified with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Leakey" title="Mary Leakey">Mary Leakey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a>, who were responsible for the preliminary archaeological research at <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyrax_Hill" title="Hyrax Hill">Hyrax Hill</a>. Later work at the former site was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Glynn_Isaac" title="Glynn Isaac">Glynn Isaac</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Isaac_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Isaac-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>East Africa, including Kenya, is one of the earliest regions where modern humans (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" class="mw-redirect" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>) are believed to have lived. Evidence was found in 2018, dating to about 320,000 years ago, at the Kenyan site of <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a>, of the early emergence of <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">modern behaviours</a> including long-distance trade networks (involving goods such as obsidian), the use of pigments, and the possible making of projectile points. It is observed by the authors of three 2018 studies on the site, that the evidence of these behaviours is approximately contemporary to the earliest known <i>Homo sapiens</i> fossil remains (such as at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a> in Morocco and <a href="/wiki/Florisbad_Skull" title="Florisbad Skull">Florisbad</a> in South Africa), and they suggest that complex and modern behaviours had already begun in Africa around the time of the emergence of the species <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR-593591796_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-593591796-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Atlantic-555674_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Atlantic-555674-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brooks_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brooks-30">[30]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span></h3> 
      <p>The first inhabitants of present-day Kenya were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> groups, akin to the modern <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretC18_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-31">[31]</a></sup> These people were later largely replaced by agropastoralist <a href="/wiki/Cushitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cushitic">Cushitic</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Cushitic speakers) from the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretHRSC_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretHRSC-32">[32]</a></sup> During the early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, the regional climate shifted from dry to wetter conditions, providing an opportunity for the development of cultural traditions such as agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Herding" title="Herding">herding</a>, in a more favourable environment.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretC18_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretC18-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Around 500 BC, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilotic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralists</a> (ancestral to Kenya's Nilotic speakers) started migrating from present-day southern Sudan into Kenya.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretCHS_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretCHS-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AmbroseALR_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseALR-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AmbroseSG_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmbroseSG-34">[34]</a></sup> Nilotic groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Kalenjin_people" title="Kalenjin people">Kalenjin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samburu_people" title="Samburu people">Samburu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luo_(family_of_ethnic_groups)" class="mw-redirect" title="Luo (family of ethnic groups)">Luo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkana_people" title="Turkana people">Turkana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By the first millennium AD, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking farmers had moved into the region, initially along the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-EhretACA_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EhretACA-36">[36]</a></sup> The Bantus originated in West Africa along the <a href="/wiki/Benue_River" title="Benue River">Benue River</a> in what is now eastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-37">[37]</a></sup> The Bantu migration brought new developments in agriculture and <a href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" class="mw-redirect" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">ironworking</a> to the region.<sup id="cite_ref-WayneSmith_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WayneSmith-37">[37]</a></sup> Bantu groups in Kenya include the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luhya_people" title="Luhya people">Luhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamba_people" title="Kamba people">Kamba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gusii_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Gusii people">Kisii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meru_people" title="Meru people">Meru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuria_people" title="Kuria people">Kuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aembu" class="mw-redirect" title="Aembu">Aembu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambeere" class="mw-redirect" title="Ambeere">Ambeere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wadawida" title="Wadawida">Wadawida</a>-Watuweta, Wapokomo, and <a href="/wiki/Mijikenda_peoples" title="Mijikenda peoples">Mijikenda</a>, among others. </p>
      <p>Notable prehistoric sites in the interior of Kenya include the (possibly archaeoastronomical) site <a href="/wiki/Namoratunga" class="mw-redirect" title="Namoratunga">Namoratunga</a> on the west side of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> and the walled settlement of <a href="/wiki/Thimlich_Ohinga" title="Thimlich Ohinga">Thimlich Ohinga</a> in <a href="/wiki/Migori_County" title="Migori County">Migori County</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_trade_period">Swahili trade period</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/220px-Lamu_door.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/330px-Lamu_door.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lamu_door.jpg/440px-Lamu_door.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1444" data-file-height="2012"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lamu_door.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A traditional <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili</a> carved wooden door in <a href="/wiki/Lamu" title="Lamu">Lamu</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Kenyan coast had served host to communities of <a href="/wiki/Ironwork" title="Ironwork">ironworkers</a> and Bantu subsistence farmers, hunters, and fishers who supported the economy with agriculture, fishing, metal production, and trade with foreign countries. These communities formed the earliest city-states in the region, which were collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pbs.org_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By the 1st century CE, many of the <a href="/wiki/City-states" class="mw-redirect" title="City-states">city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> began to establish trading relations with <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. This led to increased economic growth of the Swahili states, the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> influences on the Swahili <a href="/wiki/Bantu_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Bantu language">Bantu language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diffusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural diffusion">cultural diffusion</a>, as well as the Swahili city-states becoming members of a larger trade network.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nanjira_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nanjira-40">[40]</a></sup> Many historians had long believed that the city-states were established by Arab or Persian traders, but archaeological evidence has led scholars to recognise the city-states as an indigenous development which, though subjected to foreign influence due to trade, retained a Bantu cultural core.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa Sultanate</a> was a medieval <a href="/wiki/Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate">sultanate</a> centred at <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a>, in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. At its height, its authority stretched over the entire length of the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>, including Kenya. It was said to be founded in the 10th century by <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Hassan_Shirazi" title="Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi">Ali ibn al-Hassan Shirazi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persian</a> Sultan from <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a> in southern Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> However, scholars have suggested that claims of Arab or Persian origin of city-states were attempts by the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> to legitimise themselves both locally and internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Since the 10th century, rulers of Kilwa would go on to build elaborate coral mosques and introduce copper coinage.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Swahili, a Bantu language with <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and other Middle-Eastern and South Asian <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>, later developed as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for trade between the different peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-pbs.org_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-38">[38]</a></sup> Swahili now also has loanwords from English. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Portuguese_colonization">Early Portuguese colonization</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/220px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/330px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg/440px-Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="706" data-file-height="629"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Braun_Mombasa_UBHD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese presence</a> in Kenya lasted from 1498 until 1730. <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> was under Portuguese rule from 1593 to 1698 and again from 1728 to 1729.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Swahili built Mombasa into a major port city and established trade links with other nearby city-states, as well as commercial centres in Persia, Arabia, and even India.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> By the 15th-century, Portuguese voyager <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a> claimed that "Mombasa is a place of great traffic and has a good harbour in which there are always moored small craft of many kinds and also great ships, both of which are bound from Sofala and others which come from Cambay and Melinde and others which sail to the island of Zanzibar."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 17th century, the Swahili coast was conquered and came under the direct rule of the <a href="/wiki/Omani_Arabs" class="mw-redirect" title="Omani Arabs">Omani Arabs</a>, who expanded the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_slave_trade" title="Indian Ocean slave trade">slave trade</a> to meet the demands of plantations in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Initially, these traders came mainly from Oman, but later many came from Zanzibar (such as <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In addition, the Portuguese started buying slaves from the Omani and Zanzibari traders in response to the interruption of the transatlantic slave trade by British abolitionists. </p>
      <p>Throughout the centuries, the Kenyan coast has played host to many merchants and explorers. Among the cities that line the Kenyan coast is Malindi. It has remained an important Swahili settlement since the 14th century and once rivalled Mombasa for dominance in the African Great Lakes region. Malindi has traditionally been a friendly port city for foreign powers. In 1414, the Chinese trader and explorer <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>, representing the <a href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>, visited the East African coast <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasure voyages">on one of his last 'treasure voyages'</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Malindi authorities also welcomed the Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> in 1498. </p> 
      <h3><span id="British_Kenya_.281888.E2.80.931962.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Kenya_(1888–1962)">British Kenya (1888–1962)</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Colony" title="Kenya Colony">Kenya Colony</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/220px-Africa_1909_16a.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/330px-Africa_1909_16a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Africa_1909_16a.png/440px-Africa_1909_16a.png 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="679"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Africa_1909_16a.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> in 1909
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The colonial history of Kenya dates from the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> protectorate over the Sultan of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>'s coastal possessions in 1885, followed by the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_British_East_Africa_Company" title="Imperial British East Africa Company">Imperial British East Africa Company</a> in 1888. Imperial rivalry was prevented when Germany handed its coastal holdings to Britain in 1890. This was followed by the building of the <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Uganda Railway</a> passing through the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The building of the railway was resisted by some ethnic groups—notably the <a href="/wiki/Nandi_people" title="Nandi people">Nandi</a>, led by <i><a href="/wiki/Orkoiyot" title="Orkoiyot">Orkoiyot</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Koitalel_Arap_Samoei" title="Koitalel Arap Samoei">Koitalel Arap Samoei</a> for ten years from 1890 to 1900—however the British eventually built the railway. The <i>Nandi</i> were the first ethnic group to be put in a native reserve to stop them from disrupting the building of the railway.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the railway construction era, there was a significant influx of Indian workers, who provided the bulk of the skilled manpower required for construction.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-53">[53]</a></sup> They and most of their descendants later remained in Kenya and formed the core of several distinct Indian communities such as the <a href="/wiki/Ismailism" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismailism">Ismaili Muslim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> communities.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While building the railway through <a href="/wiki/Tsavo" title="Tsavo">Tsavo</a>, a number of the Indian railway workers and local African labourers were attacked by two <a href="/wiki/Lions" class="mw-redirect" title="Lions">lions</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Tsavo_maneaters" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsavo maneaters">Tsavo maneaters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At the outbreak of World War I in August 1914, the governors of <a href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> (as the protectorate was generally known) and <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> initially agreed on a truce in an attempt to keep the young colonies out of direct hostilities. <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Lettow-Vorbeck" title="Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck">Lt. Col. Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck</a> took command of the German military forces, determined to tie down as many British resources as possible. Completely cut off from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Lettow-Vorbeck" title="Paul von Lettow-Vorbeck">von Lettow</a> conducted an effective <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a> campaign, living off the land, capturing British supplies, and remaining undefeated. He eventually surrendered in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> (today Zambia) fourteen days after the Armistice was signed in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-53">[53]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/300px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/450px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg/600px-Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="821" data-file-height="339"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Kurve_bei_Mombasa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Railway" title="Uganda Railway">Kenya–Uganda Railway</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, about 1899.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>To chase von Lettow, the British deployed the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Army" title="British Indian Army">British Indian Army</a> troops from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a> but needed large numbers of porters to overcome the formidable logistics of transporting supplies far into the interior on foot. The <a href="/wiki/Carrier_Corps" title="Carrier Corps">Carrier Corps</a> was formed and ultimately mobilised over 400,000 Africans, contributing to their long-term politicisation.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1920, the East Africa Protectorate was turned into a colony and renamed Kenya after its highest mountain.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the early part of the 20th century, the interior central highlands were settled by British and other European farmers, who became wealthy farming coffee and tea.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> (one depiction of this period of change from one colonist's perspective is found in the memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa" title="Out of Africa">Out of Africa</a></i> by Danish author Baroness Karen von Blixen-Finecke, published in 1937). By the 1930s, approximately 30,000 white settlers lived in the area and gained a political voice because of their contribution to the market economy.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The central highlands were already home to over a million members of the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu people</a>, most of whom had no land claims in European terms and lived as itinerant farmers. To protect their interests, the settlers banned the growing of coffee, introduced a hut tax, and the landless were granted less and less land in exchange for their labour. A massive exodus to the cities ensued as their ability to provide a living from the land dwindled.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mugo_Gatheru_2005-53">[53]</a></sup> By the 1950s, there were 80,000 <a href="/wiki/Whites_in_Kenya" class="mw-redirect" title="Whites in Kenya">white settlers</a> living in Kenya .<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya_in_World_War_II" title="Kenya in World War II">Kenya was</a> an important source of manpower and agriculture for the United Kingdom. Kenya itself was the site of <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">fighting</a> between Allied forces and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italian</a> troops in 1940–41, when Italian forces invaded. <a href="/wiki/Wajir" title="Wajir">Wajir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> were bombed as well. </p>
      <p>In 1952, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Princess Elizabeth</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip</a> were on holiday at the <a href="/wiki/Treetops_Hotel" title="Treetops Hotel">Treetops Hotel</a> in Kenya when her father, <a href="/wiki/King_George_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="King George VI">King George VI</a>, died in his sleep. The young princess cut short her trip and returned home immediately to assume the throne. She was crowned Queen Elizabeth II at Westminster Abbey in 1953 and as British hunter and conservationist <a href="/wiki/Jim_Corbett" title="Jim Corbett">Jim Corbett</a> (who accompanied the royal couple) put it, she went up a tree in Africa a princess and came down a queen.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mau_Mau_Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi%2C_Kenya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2176"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dedan_Kimathi_Nairobi,_Kenya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A statue of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a>, a Kenyan rebel leader with the <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau" class="mw-redirect" title="Mau Mau">Mau Mau</a> who fought against the British colonial system in the 1950s.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>From October 1952 to December 1959, Kenya was in a state of emergency arising from the <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau rebellion</a> against British rule. The Mau Mau, also known as the Kenya Land and Freedom Army, were primarily members of the Kikuyu ethnic group.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The governor requested and obtained British and African troops, including the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_African_Rifles" title="King's African Rifles">King's African Rifles</a>. The British began <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> operations. In May 1953, General Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Erskine" title="George Erskine">George Erskine</a> took charge as commander-in-chief of the colony's armed forces, with the personal backing of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The capture of <a href="/wiki/Waruhiu_Itote" title="Waruhiu Itote">Waruhiu Itote</a> (nom de guerre <i>"General China"</i>) on 15 January 1954 and the subsequent interrogation led to a better understanding of the Mau Mau command structure for the British. <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising#British_gain_the_initiative" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Operation Anvil</a> opened on 24 April 1954, after weeks of planning by the army with the approval of the War Council. The operation effectively placed Nairobi under military siege. Nairobi's occupants were screened and the suspected Mau Mau supporters moved to <a href="/wiki/Detention_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Detention camp">detention camps</a>. More than 80,000 members of the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a> ethnic group were <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Detention_Camps_during_the_Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="List of British Detention Camps during the Mau Mau Uprising">held in detention camps</a> without trial, often subject to brutal treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The Home Guard formed the core of the government's strategy as it was composed of loyalist Africans, not foreign forces such as the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and King's African Rifles. By the end of the emergency, the Home Guard had killed 4,686 Mau Mau, amounting to 42% of the total insurgents. </p>
      <p>The capture of <a href="/wiki/Dedan_Kimathi" title="Dedan Kimathi">Dedan Kimathi</a> on 21 October 1956 in <a href="/wiki/Nyeri" title="Nyeri">Nyeri</a> signified the ultimate defeat of the Mau Mau and essentially ended the military offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wunyabari_O._Maloba_1993-59">[59]</a></sup> During this period, substantial governmental changes to land tenure occurred. The most important of these was the <a href="/wiki/Swynnerton_Plan" title="Swynnerton Plan">Swynnerton Plan</a>, which was used to both reward loyalists and punish Mau Mau. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Somalis_of_Kenya_referendum.2C_1962"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Somalis_of_Kenya_referendum,_1962">Somalis of Kenya referendum, 1962</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somalis in Kenya</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Before Kenya got its independence, <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somali ethnic people in the present-day Kenya</a> in the areas of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_Districts" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Frontier Districts">Northern Frontier Districts</a> petitioned Her Majesty's Government not to be included in Kenya. The colonial government decided to hold Kenya's first referendum in 1962 to check the willingness of <a href="/wiki/Somalis_in_Kenya" title="Somalis in Kenya">Somalis in Kenya</a> to join <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The result of the referendum showed that 86% of Somalis in Kenya wanted to join <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, but the British colonial administration rejected the result and the Somalis remained in Kenya.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg/170px-Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="255" data-file-height="366"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jomo_Kenyatta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The first president and founding father of Kenya, <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first direct elections for native Kenyans to the Legislative Council took place in 1957. </p>
      <p>Despite British hopes of handing power to "moderate" local rivals, it was the <a href="/wiki/Kenya_African_National_Union" title="Kenya African National Union">Kenya African National Union</a> (KANU) of <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> that formed a government. The Colony of Kenya and the Protectorate of Kenya each came to an end on 12 December 1963, with independence being conferred on all of Kenya. The United Kingdom ceded sovereignty over the Colony of Kenya. The Sultan of Zanzibar agreed that simultaneous with independence for the colony, the sultan would cease to have sovereignty over the Protectorate of Kenya so that all of Kenya would become one sovereign state.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In this way, Kenya became an independent country under the Kenya Independence Act 1963 of the United Kingdom. Exactly 12 months later on 12 December 1964, Kenya became a republic under the name "Republic of Kenya".<sup id="cite_ref-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_Roberts-Wray_1966._P._762-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Concurrently, the Kenyan army fought the <a href="/wiki/Shifta_War" title="Shifta War">Shifta War</a> against ethnic <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a> rebels inhabiting the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Frontier_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Frontier District">Northern Frontier District</a> who wanted to join their kin in the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Republic" title="Somali Republic">Somali Republic</a> to the north.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A ceasefire was eventually reached with the signature of the Arusha Memorandum in October 1967, but relative insecurity prevailed through 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogg1986_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1986-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howell1968_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howell1968-68">[68]</a></sup> To discourage further invasions, Kenya signed a defence pact with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1969, which is still in effect.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_president_of_Kenya">The first president of Kenya</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta">Presidency of Jomo Kenyatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 12 December 1964, the Republic of Kenya was proclaimed, and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> became Kenya's first president.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Under Kenyatta, corruption became widespread throughout the government, civil service, and business community. Kenyatta and his family were tied up with this corruption as they enriched themselves through the mass purchase of property after 1963. Their acquisitions in the Central, Rift Valley, and Coast Provinces aroused great anger among landless Kenyans. His family used his presidential position to circumvent legal or administrative obstacles to acquiring property. The Kenyatta family also heavily invested in the coastal hotel business, with Kenyatta personally owning the Leonard Beach Hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>President Jomo Kenyatta's mixed legacy was highlighted at the 10 year anniversary of Kenya's independence. An article published in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a> in December 1973 praised Kenyatta's leadership and Kenya for emerging as a model of pragmatism and conservatism. Kenya's GDP had increased at a rate of 6.6 per cent a year, higher than the population growth rate of more than 3 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> responded to this article by stating the cost of the stability in terms of human rights abuses. The opposition party started by <a href="/wiki/Oginga_Odinga" class="mw-redirect" title="Oginga Odinga">Oginga Odinga</a> - <a href="/wiki/Kenya_People%27s_Union" title="Kenya People's Union">Kenya People's Union</a> (KPU) - was banned in 1969 following the <a href="/wiki/Kisumu_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Kisumu Massacre">Kisumu Massacre</a> and KPU leaders were still in detention without trial in gross violation of the <a href="/wiki/U.N._Declaration_of_Human_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="U.N. Declaration of Human Rights">U.N. Declaration of Human Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sproul_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sproul-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The Kenya Students Union, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah_Witnesses" class="mw-redirect" title="Jehovah Witnesses">Jehovah Witnesses</a> and all opposition parties were outlawed.<sup id="cite_ref-sproul_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sproul-73">[73]</a></sup> Kenyatta ruled until his death on 22 August 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moi_era">Moi era</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg/220px-Moi_and_Bush.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Moi_and_Bush.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="298" data-file-height="221"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Moi_and_Bush.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, Kenya's second President, and George W. Bush, 2001
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Daniel_Moi" title="Presidency of Daniel Moi">Presidency of Daniel Moi</a>, <a href="/wiki/1978_Kenyan_presidential_election" title="1978 Kenyan presidential election">1978 Kenyan presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/1988_Kenyan_general_election" title="1988 Kenyan general election">1988 Kenyan general election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1992_Kenyan_general_election" title="1992 Kenyan general election">1992 Kenyan general election</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Following Kenyatta's death in 1978, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_arap_Moi" title="Daniel arap Moi">Daniel arap Moi</a> became president. He retained the presidency, running unopposed in elections held in 1979, 1983 (<a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap elections</a>), and 1988, all of which were held under the single-party constitution. The 1983 elections were held a year early, and were a direct result of <a href="/wiki/1982_Kenyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1982 Kenyan coup d'état attempt">a failed military coup</a> on 2 August 1982. </p>
      <p>The 1982 coup was masterminded by a low-ranking Air Force serviceman, Senior Private <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah_Ochuka" title="Hezekiah Ochuka">Hezekiah Ochuka</a>, and was staged mainly by enlisted men of the Air Force. It was quickly suppressed by forces commanded by Chief of General Staff <a href="/wiki/Mahamoud_Mohamed" title="Mahamoud Mohamed">Mahamoud Mohamed</a>, a veteran Somali military official.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> They included the General Service Unit (GSU)—a paramilitary wing of the police—and later the regular police. </p>
      <p>On the heels of the <a href="/wiki/Garissa_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Garissa Massacre">Garissa Massacre</a> of 1980, Kenyan troops committed the <a href="/wiki/Wagalla_massacre" title="Wagalla massacre">Wagalla massacre</a> in 1984 against thousands of civilians in <a href="/wiki/Wajir_County" title="Wajir County">Wajir County</a>. An official probe into the atrocities was later ordered in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The election held in 1988 saw the advent of the <i>mlolongo</i> (queuing) system, where voters were supposed to line up behind their favoured candidates instead of casting a secret ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> This was seen as the climax of a very undemocratic regime and it led to widespread agitation for constitutional reform. Several contentious clauses, including the one that allowed for only one political party, were changed in the following years.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_multiparty_democracy">Transition to multiparty democracy</span></h3> 
      <p>In 1991, Kenya transitioned to a multiparty political system after 26 years of single-party rule. On 28 October 1992, president Moi dissolved parliament, five months before the end of his term. As a result, preparations began for all elective seats in parliament as well as the president. The elections were scheduled to take place on 7 December 1992, but delays led to its postponement to 29 December the same year. Apart from KANU, the ruling party, other parties represented in the elections included <a href="/wiki/Forum_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Forum for the Restoration of Democracy">FORD</a> Kenya and FORD Asili. This election was marked by large-scale intimidation of opponents, as well as harassment of election officials. It resulted in an economic crisis propagated by ethnic violence as the president was accused of rigging electoral results to retain power.<sup id="cite_ref-Keith_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldsworthy_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldsworthy-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-82">[82]</a></sup> This election was a turning point for Kenya as it signified the beginning of the end of Moi's leadership and the rule of KANU. Moi retained the presidency and <a href="/wiki/George_Saitoti" title="George Saitoti">George Saitoti</a> became the vice-president. Although it held on to power, KANU won 100 seats and lost 88 seats to the six opposition parties.<sup id="cite_ref-Keith_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ParliamentKe_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParliamentKe-82">[82]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <td><b>Round no 1 (29 December 1992): Election results</b> </td>

      <table class="wikitable">

        </tr>
